But whether the affliction was rheumatism, a stroke or a nervous breakdown, he recovered remarkably quickly .<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> To aid his recovery, Handel had travelled to <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a>, a spa in Germany. During six weeks he took long hot baths, and ended up playing the organ for a surprised audience.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> It was even possible for him to write one of his most popular operas, <i><a href="/wiki/Serse" title="Serse">Serse</a></i> (including the famous aria <a href="/wiki/Ombra_mai_f%C3%B9" class="mw-redirect" title="Ombra mai fù">Ombra mai fù</a>, better known as "Handel's largo", which he wrote for the famous castrato <a href="/wiki/Caffarelli_(castrato)" title="Caffarelli (castrato)">Caffarelli</a>), just one year after his stroke.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Deidamia_(opera)" title="Deidamia (opera)">Deidamia</a></i>, his last opera, a co-production with the <a href="/wiki/Robert_Darcy,_4th_Earl_of_Holderness" title="Robert Darcy, 4th Earl of Holderness">Earl of Holderness</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> was performed three times in 1741. Handel gave up the opera business, while he enjoyed more success with his English oratorios.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHicks2013_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHicks2013-135">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oratorio">Oratorio</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Oratorio">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_George_Frideric_Handel#Oratorios" title="List of compositions by George Frideric Handel">List of compositions by George Frideric Handel §&#160;Oratorios</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Retrato_de_Handel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Retrato_de_Handel.jpg/170px-Retrato_de_Handel.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Retrato_de_Handel.jpg/255px-Retrato_de_Handel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Retrato_de_Handel.jpg/340px-Retrato_de_Handel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1503" data-file-height="2223" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Retrato_de_Handel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting of Handel by <a href="/wiki/Philip_Mercier" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip Mercier">Philip Mercier</a></div></div></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Il_trionfo_del_tempo_e_del_disinganno" class="mw-redirect" title="Il trionfo del tempo e del disinganno">Il trionfo del tempo e del disinganno</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegory</a>, Handel's first oratorio<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarx1998243_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx1998243-136">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> was composed in Italy in 1707, followed by <i><a href="/wiki/La_resurrezione" title="La resurrezione">La resurrezione</a></i> in 1708 which uses material from the Bible. The circumstances of <i><a href="/wiki/Esther_(Handel)" title="Esther (Handel)">Esther</a></i> and its first performance, possibly in 1718, are obscure.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Another 12 years had passed when an act of piracy caused him to take up <i>Esther</i> once again.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Three earlier performances aroused such interest that they naturally prompted the idea of introducing it to a larger public. Next came <i><a href="/wiki/Deborah_(Handel)" title="Deborah (Handel)">Deborah</a></i>, strongly coloured by the coronation anthems<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Athalia_(Handel)" title="Athalia (Handel)">Athaliah</a></i>, his first English Oratorio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarx199848_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx199848-140">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> In these three oratorios Handel laid the foundation for the traditional use of the chorus which marks his later oratorios.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Handel became sure of himself, broader in his presentation, and more diverse in his composition.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is evident how much he learned from <a href="/wiki/Arcangelo_Corelli" title="Arcangelo Corelli">Arcangelo Corelli</a> about writing for instruments, and from <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Scarlatti" title="Alessandro Scarlatti">Alessandro Scarlatti</a> about writing for the solo voice; but there is no single composer who taught him how to write for chorus.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Handel tended more and more to replace Italian soloists by English ones. The most significant reason for this change was the dwindling financial returns from his operas.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Thus a tradition was created for oratorios which was to govern their future performance. The performances were given without costumes and action; the singers appeared in their own clothes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrows1994217_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrows1994217-145">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Goupy,_1754_-_GFHandel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Joseph_Goupy%2C_1754_-_GFHandel.jpg/220px-Joseph_Goupy%2C_1754_-_GFHandel.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Joseph_Goupy%2C_1754_-_GFHandel.jpg/330px-Joseph_Goupy%2C_1754_-_GFHandel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Joseph_Goupy%2C_1754_-_GFHandel.jpg/440px-Joseph_Goupy%2C_1754_-_GFHandel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="706" data-file-height="925" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Goupy,_1754_-_GFHandel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Caricature" title="Caricature">Caricature</a> of Handel by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goupy" title="Joseph Goupy">Joseph Goupy</a> (1754)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1736 Handel produced <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander%27s_Feast_(Handel)" title="Alexander&#39;s Feast (Handel)">Alexander's Feast</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/John_Beard_(tenor)" title="John Beard (tenor)">John Beard</a> appeared for the first time as one of Handel's principal singers and became Handel's permanent tenor soloist for the rest of Handel's life.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The piece was a great success and it encouraged Handel to make the transition from writing Italian operas to English choral works. In <i><a href="/wiki/Saul_(Handel)" title="Saul (Handel)">Saul</a></i>, Handel was collaborating with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Jennens" title="Charles Jennens">Charles Jennens</a> and experimenting with three trombones, a carillon and extra-large military kettledrums (from the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>), to be sure "...it will be most excessive noisy".<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> <i>Saul</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Israel_in_Egypt" title="Israel in Egypt">Israel in Egypt</a></i> both from 1739 head the list of great, mature oratorios, in which the da capo aria became the exception and not the rule.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> <i>Israel in Egypt</i> consists of little else but choruses, borrowing from the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ways_of_Zion_Do_Mourn_/_Funeral_Anthem_for_Queen_Caroline" title="The Ways of Zion Do Mourn / Funeral Anthem for Queen Caroline">Funeral Anthem for Queen Caroline</a></i>. In his next works Handel changed his course. In these works he laid greater stress on the effects of orchestra and soloists; the chorus retired into the background.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Allegro,_il_Penseroso_ed_il_Moderato" title="L&#39;Allegro, il Penseroso ed il Moderato">L'Allegro, il Penseroso ed il Moderato</a></i> has a rather diverting character; the work is light and fresh.
</p><p>During the summer of 1741, <a href="/wiki/William_Cavendish,_3rd_Duke_of_Devonshire" title="William Cavendish, 3rd Duke of Devonshire">The 3rd Duke of Devonshire</a> invited Handel to <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, capital of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a>, to give concerts for the benefit of local hospitals.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> His <i><a href="/wiki/Messiah_(Handel)" title="Messiah (Handel)">Messiah</a></i> was first performed at the New Music Hall in <a href="/wiki/Fishamble_Street" title="Fishamble Street">Fishamble Street</a> on 13 April 1742, with 26 boys and five men from the combined choirs of <a href="/wiki/St_Patrick%27s_Cathedral,_Dublin" title="St Patrick&#39;s Cathedral, Dublin">St Patrick's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church_Cathedral,_Dublin" title="Christ Church Cathedral, Dublin">Christ Church</a> cathedrals participating.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Handel secured a balance between soloists and chorus which he never surpassed.
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Handel_-_messiah_-_44_hallelujah.ogg" title="File:Handel - messiah - 44 hallelujah.ogg">Hallelujah Chorus, from <i>Messiah</i> (1741)</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="227.38553287982" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Handel_-_messiah_-_44_hallelujah.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Handel_-_messiah_-_44_hallelujah.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (119 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="119201" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/ce/Handel_-_messiah_-_44_hallelujah.ogg/Handel_-_messiah_-_44_hallelujah.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="211168" /></audio></div></div>

<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="200.52" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Handel_-_Arrival_of_the_Queen_of_Sheba.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/2d/Handel_-_Arrival_of_the_Queen_of_Sheba.ogg/Handel_-_Arrival_of_the_Queen_of_Sheba.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="216272" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2d/Handel_-_Arrival_of_the_Queen_of_Sheba.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (228 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="227573" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing these files? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>In 1747 Handel wrote his oratorio <i>Alexander Balus</i>. This work was produced at Covent Garden Theatre in London, on 23 March 1748, and to the aria <i>Hark! hark! He strikes the golden lyre</i>, Handel wrote the accompaniment for <a href="/wiki/Mandolin" title="Mandolin">mandolin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Violoncello" class="mw-redirect" title="Violoncello">violoncello</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBone1914142_44_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBone1914142_44-152">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Another of his English oratorios, <i><a href="/wiki/Solomon_(Handel)" title="Solomon (Handel)">Solomon</a></i>, was first performed on 17 March 1749 at the Covent Garden Theatre.<sup id="cite_ref-Handel_Institute_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handel_Institute-153">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> <i>Solomon</i> contains a short and lively instrumental passage for two oboes and strings in act 3, known as "The Arrival of the Queen of Sheba".
</p><p>The use of English soloists reached its height at the first performance of <i><a href="/wiki/Samson_(Handel)" title="Samson (Handel)">Samson</a></i>. The work is highly theatrical. The role of the chorus became increasingly important in his later oratorios. <i><a href="/wiki/Jephtha_(Handel)" title="Jephtha (Handel)">Jephtha</a></i> was first performed on 26 February 1752; even though it was his last oratorio, it was no less a masterpiece than his earlier works.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Later years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haendel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Haendel.jpg/170px-Haendel.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Haendel.jpg/255px-Haendel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Haendel.jpg/340px-Haendel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="917" data-file-height="1150" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haendel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Handel in 1733, by <a href="/wiki/Balthasar_Denner" title="Balthasar Denner">Balthasar Denner</a> (1685–1749)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1749 Handel composed <i><a href="/wiki/Music_for_the_Royal_Fireworks" title="Music for the Royal Fireworks">Music for the Royal Fireworks</a></i>; 12,000 people attended the first performance.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In 1750 he arranged a performance of <i>Messiah</i> to benefit the <a href="/wiki/Foundling_Hospital" title="Foundling Hospital">Foundling Hospital</a>. The performance was considered a great success and was followed by annual concerts that continued throughout his life. In recognition of his patronage, Handel was made a governor of the Hospital the day after his initial concert. He bequeathed a copy of <i>Messiah</i> to the institution upon his death.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> His involvement with the Foundling Hospital is today commemorated with a permanent exhibition in London's <a href="/wiki/Foundling_Museum" title="Foundling Museum">Foundling Museum</a>, which also holds the <i>Gerald Coke Handel Collection</i>. In addition to the Foundling Hospital, Handel also gave to a charity that assisted impoverished musicians and their families.
</p><p>In August 1750, on a journey back from Germany to London, Handel was seriously injured in a carriage accident between <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haarlem" title="Haarlem">Haarlem</a> in the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> In 1751 one eye started to fail. The cause was a <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataract</a> which was operated on by the great charlatan <a href="/wiki/John_Taylor_(oculist)" title="John Taylor (oculist)">Chevalier Taylor</a>. This did not improve his eyesight, but possibly made it worse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHicks2013_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHicks2013-135">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> He was completely blind by 1752. He died in 1759 at home in Brook Street, at age 74. The last performance he attended was of <i>Messiah</i>. Handel was buried in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> More than three thousand mourners attended his funeral, which was given full state honours.
</p><p>Handel never married, and kept his personal life private. His initial <a href="/wiki/Will_of_George_Frideric_Handel" title="Will of George Frideric Handel">will</a> bequeathed the bulk of his estate to his niece Johanna, however four <a href="/wiki/Codicil_(will)" title="Codicil (will)">codicils</a> distributed much of his estate to other relations, servants, friends and charities.<sup id="cite_ref-Muller_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muller-159">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Handel owned an <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel%27s_art_collection" title="George Frideric Handel&#39;s art collection">art collection</a> that was auctioned posthumously in 1760.<sup id="cite_ref-em.oxfordjournals.com_–_McGeary_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-em.oxfordjournals.com_–_McGeary-160">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The auction catalogue listed approximately seventy paintings and ten prints (other paintings were bequeathed).<sup id="cite_ref-em.oxfordjournals.com_–_McGeary_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-em.oxfordjournals.com_–_McGeary-160">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_George_Frideric_Handel" title="List of compositions by George Frideric Handel">List of compositions by George Frideric Handel</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_operas_by_George_Frideric_Handel" title="List of operas by George Frideric Handel">List of operas by George Frideric Handel</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Senesino.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Senesino.jpg/170px-Senesino.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Senesino.jpg/255px-Senesino.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/Senesino.jpg 2x" data-file-width="339" data-file-height="486" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Senesino.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Senesino" title="Senesino">Senesino</a>, the famous <a href="/wiki/Castrato" title="Castrato">castrato</a> from <a href="/wiki/Siena" title="Siena">Siena</a></div></div></div>
<p>Handel's compositions include 42 operas, 29 oratorios, more than 120 cantatas, trios and duets, numerous arias, chamber music, a large number of ecumenical pieces, odes and serenatas, and 16 organ concerti. His most famous work, the oratorio <i><a href="/wiki/Messiah_(Handel)" title="Messiah (Handel)">Messiah</a></i> with its "Hallelujah" chorus, is among the most popular works in choral music and has become the centrepiece of the Christmas season. The <a href="/wiki/Lobkowicz_Palace" title="Lobkowicz Palace">Lobkowicz Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> holds <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart's</a> copy of <i>Messiah</i>, complete with handwritten annotations. Among the works with opus numbers published and popularised in his lifetime are the <a href="/wiki/Organ_concertos,_Op._4_(Handel)" title="Organ concertos, Op. 4 (Handel)">Organ Concertos Op. 4</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organ_concertos,_Op._7_(Handel)" title="Organ concertos, Op. 7 (Handel)">Op. 7</a>, together with the Opus 3 and <a href="/wiki/Concerti_grossi,_Op._6_(Handel)" title="Concerti grossi, Op. 6 (Handel)">Opus 6 concerti grossi</a>; the latter incorporate an earlier organ concerto <i>The Cuckoo and the Nightingale</i> in which birdsong is imitated in the upper registers of the organ. Also notable are his sixteen keyboard suites, especially <i><a href="/wiki/The_Harmonious_Blacksmith" title="The Harmonious Blacksmith">The Harmonious Blacksmith</a></i>.
</p><p>Handel introduced previously uncommon musical instruments in his works: the <a href="/wiki/Viola_d%27amore" title="Viola d&#39;amore">viola d'amore</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Violetta_marina&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Violetta marina (page does not exist)">violetta marina</a> (<i>Orlando</i>), the lute (<i>Ode for St. Cecilia's Day</i>), three trombones (<i>Saul</i>), clarinets or small high cornetts (<i>Tamerlano</i>), <a href="/wiki/Theorbo" title="Theorbo">theorbo</a>, French horn (<i><a href="/wiki/Water_Music" title="Water Music">Water Music</a></i>), lyrichord, <a href="/wiki/Double_bassoon" class="mw-redirect" title="Double bassoon">double bassoon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viola_da_gamba" class="mw-redirect" title="Viola da gamba">viola da gamba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carillon" title="Carillon">carillon</a> (bell chimes), <a href="/wiki/Positive_organ" title="Positive organ">positive organ</a>, and harp (<i>Giulio Cesare</i>, <i>Alexander's Feast</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catalogues">Catalogues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Catalogues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first published catalogue of Handel's works appeared as an appendix to Mainwaring's <i>Memoirs</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMainwaring1760145–55_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMainwaring1760145–55-162">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Between 1787 and 1797 <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Arnold_(composer)" title="Samuel Arnold (composer)">Samuel Arnold</a> compiled a 180-volume collection of Handel's works—however, it was far from complete.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean1982116_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean1982116-163">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Also incomplete was the collection produced between 1843 and 1858 by the English Handel Society (founded by <a href="/wiki/George_Alexander_Macfarren" title="George Alexander Macfarren">Sir George Macfarren</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 105-volume <i><a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4ndel-Gesellschaft" title="Händel-Gesellschaft">Händel-Gesellschaft</a></i> ("Handel Society") edition was published between 1858 and 1902 – mainly due to the efforts of <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Chrysander" title="Friedrich Chrysander">Friedrich Chrysander</a>. For modern performance, the realisation of the basso continuo reflects 19th century practice. Vocal scores drawn from the edition were published by <a href="/wiki/Novello_%26_Co" class="mw-redirect" title="Novello &amp; Co">Novello</a> in London, but some scores, such as the vocal score to <i>Samson</i>, are incomplete.
</p><p>The continuing <i><a href="/wiki/Hallische_H%C3%A4ndel-Ausgabe" title="Hallische Händel-Ausgabe">Hallische Händel-Ausgabe</a></i> edition was first inaugurated in 1955 in the <a href="/wiki/Halle_(region)" title="Halle (region)">Halle</a> region in <a href="/wiki/Saxony-Anhalt" title="Saxony-Anhalt">Saxony-Anhalt</a>, Eastern Germany. It did not start as a critical edition, but after heavy criticism of the first volumes, which were performing editions without a critical apparatus (for example, the opera <i>Serse</i> was published with the title character recast as a tenor, reflecting pre-war German practice), it repositioned itself as a critical edition. Influenced in part by cold-war realities, editorial work was inconsistent: misprints are found in abundance and editors failed to consult important sources. In 1985 a committee was formed to establish better standards for the edition. The unification of Germany in 1990 removed communication problems, and the volumes issued have since shown a significant improvement in standards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHicks2013_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHicks2013-135">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1978 and 1986 the German academic <a href="/wiki/Bernd_Baselt" title="Bernd Baselt">Bernd Baselt</a> catalogued Handel's works in his <i><a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4ndel-Werke-Verzeichnis" title="Händel-Werke-Verzeichnis">Händel-Werke-Verzeichnis</a></i> publication. The catalogue has achieved wide acceptance and is used as the modern numbering system, with each of Handel's works designated an "HWV" number, for example <i>Messiah</i> is catalogued as "HWV 56".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Masquerade_at_the_King%27s_Theatre_Haymarket_c1724.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Masquerade_at_the_King%27s_Theatre_Haymarket_c1724.jpg/220px-Masquerade_at_the_King%27s_Theatre_Haymarket_c1724.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Masquerade_at_the_King%27s_Theatre_Haymarket_c1724.jpg/330px-Masquerade_at_the_King%27s_Theatre_Haymarket_c1724.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Masquerade_at_the_King%27s_Theatre_Haymarket_c1724.jpg/440px-Masquerade_at_the_King%27s_Theatre_Haymarket_c1724.jpg 2x" data-file-width="758" data-file-height="572" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Masquerade_at_the_King%27s_Theatre_Haymarket_c1724.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Masquerade at the King's Theatre, Haymarket (c. 1724)</div></div></div>
<p>Handel's works were collected and preserved by two men: <a href="/wiki/Sir_Samuel_Hellier" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Samuel Hellier">Sir Samuel Hellier</a>, a country squire whose musical acquisitions form the nucleus of the Shaw-Hellier Collection,<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and the abolitionist <a href="/wiki/Granville_Sharp" title="Granville Sharp">Granville Sharp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The catalogue accompanying the <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery,_London" title="National Portrait Gallery, London">National Portrait Gallery</a> exhibition marking the tercentenary of the composer's birth calls them two men of the late eighteenth century "who have left us solid evidence of the means by which they indulged their enthusiasm".<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> With his English oratorios, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Messiah_(Handel)" title="Messiah (Handel)">Messiah</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Solomon_(Handel)" title="Solomon (Handel)">Solomon</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_anthem#Handel&#39;s_coronation_anthems" title="Coronation anthem">coronation anthems</a>, and other works including <i><a href="/wiki/Water_Music" title="Water Music">Water Music</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Music_for_the_Royal_Fireworks" title="Music for the Royal Fireworks">Music for the Royal Fireworks</a></i>, Handel became a national icon in Britain, and featured in the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> series, <i>The Birth of British Music: Handel – The Conquering Hero</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HandelVA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HandelVA.jpg/170px-HandelVA.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HandelVA.jpg/255px-HandelVA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HandelVA.jpg/340px-HandelVA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="631" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HandelVA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A carved <a href="/wiki/George_Frederick_Handel_(Roubiliac)" title="George Frederick Handel (Roubiliac)">marble statue of Handel</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_and_Albert_Museum" title="Victoria and Albert Museum">Victoria and Albert Museum</a>, London, created in 1738 by <a href="/wiki/Louis-Fran%C3%A7ois_Roubiliac" title="Louis-François Roubiliac">Louis-François Roubiliac</a></div></div></div>
<p>After his death, Handel's Italian operas fell into obscurity, except for selections such as the aria from <i><a href="/wiki/Serse" title="Serse">Serse</a></i>, "<a href="/wiki/Ombra_mai_f%C3%B9" class="mw-redirect" title="Ombra mai fù">Ombra mai fù</a>". The oratorios continued to be performed but not long after Handel's death they were thought to need some modernisation, and Mozart orchestrated German versions of <i>Messiah</i> and other works. Throughout the 19th century and first half of the 20th century, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">Anglophone</a> countries, his reputation rested primarily on his English oratorios, which were customarily performed by choruses of amateur singers on solemn occasions.  The centenary of his death, in 1859, was celebrated by a performance of <i>Messiah</i> at <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">The Crystal Palace</a>, involving 2,765 singers and 460 instrumentalists, who played for an audience of about 10,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Handel_-_Zadok_the_Priest.ogg" title="File:Handel - Zadok the Priest.ogg">Zadok the Priest (1727)</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="362.28063492063" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Handel_-_Zadok_the_Priest.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/83/Handel_-_Zadok_the_Priest.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (115 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="115265" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/83/Handel_-_Zadok_the_Priest.ogg/Handel_-_Zadok_the_Priest.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="211080" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Composed for the coronation of <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">King George II</a> in 1727, <i><a href="/wiki/Zadok_the_Priest" title="Zadok the Priest">Zadok the Priest</a></i> has been performed at every subsequent <a href="/wiki/British_coronation" class="mw-redirect" title="British coronation">British coronation</a></div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Recent decades have revived his secular cantatas and what one might call 'secular oratorios' or 'concert operas'. Of the former, <i><a href="/wiki/Ode_for_St._Cecilia%27s_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Ode for St. Cecilia&#39;s Day">Ode for St. Cecilia's Day</a></i> (1739) (set to texts by <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Ode_for_the_Birthday_of_Queen_Anne" title="Ode for the Birthday of Queen Anne">Ode for the Birthday of Queen Anne</a></i> (1713) are noteworthy. For his secular oratorios, Handel turned to classical mythology for subjects, producing such works as <i><a href="/wiki/Acis_and_Galatea_(Handel)" title="Acis and Galatea (Handel)">Acis and Galatea</a></i> (1719), <i><a href="/wiki/Hercules_(music_drama)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hercules (music drama)">Hercules</a></i> (1745) and <i><a href="/wiki/Semele_(oratorio)" class="mw-redirect" title="Semele (oratorio)">Semele</a></i> (1744). These works have a close kinship with the sacred oratorios, particularly in the vocal writing for the English-language texts. They also share the lyrical and dramatic qualities of Handel's Italian operas. As such, they are sometimes fully staged as operas. With the rediscovery of his theatrical works, Handel, in addition to his renown as instrumentalist, orchestral writer, and melodist, is now perceived as being one of opera's great musical dramatists.
</p><p>The original form of his name, Georg Friedrich Händel, is generally used in Germany and elsewhere, but he is known as "Haendel" in France. A different composer, Jacob Handl or Händl (1550–1591) is usually known by the Latin form <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Gallus" title="Jacobus Gallus">Jacobus Gallus</a> that appears in his publications.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Handel has generally been accorded high esteem by fellow composers, both in his own time and since.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Bach</a> attempted, unsuccessfully, to meet Handel while he was visiting <a href="/wiki/Halle_(Saale)" title="Halle (Saale)">Halle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a> is reputed to have said of him, "Handel understands <a href="/wiki/Affekt" class="mw-redirect" title="Affekt">affect</a> better than any of us. When he chooses, he strikes like a thunder bolt."<sup id="cite_ref-YoungMM_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YoungMM-172">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> To <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Beethoven</a> he was "the master of us all... the greatest composer that ever lived. I would uncover my head and kneel before his tomb."<sup id="cite_ref-YoungMM_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YoungMM-172">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Beethoven emphasised above all the simplicity and popular appeal of Handel's music when he said, "Go to him to learn how to achieve great effects, by such simple means."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Borrowings">Borrowings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Borrowings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since 1831, when <a href="/wiki/William_Crotch" title="William Crotch">William Crotch</a> raised the issue in his <i>Substance of Several Lectures on Music,</i> scholars have extensively studied Handel's "borrowing" of music from other composers. Summarising the field in 2005, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Taruskin" title="Richard Taruskin">Richard Taruskin</a> wrote that Handel "seems to have been the champion of all <a href="/wiki/Parody_music" title="Parody music">parodists</a>, adapting both his own works and those of other composers in unparalleled numbers and with unparalleled exactitude."<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Among the composers whose music has been shown to have been re-used by Handel are <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Stradella" title="Alessandro Stradella">Alessandro Stradella</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gottlieb_Muffat" title="Gottlieb Muffat">Gottlieb Muffat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Scarlatti" title="Alessandro Scarlatti">Alessandro Scarlatti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Scarlatti" title="Domenico Scarlatti">Domenico Scarlatti</a><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Giacomo_Carissimi" title="Giacomo Carissimi">Giacomo Carissimi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georg_Philipp_Telemann" title="Georg Philipp Telemann">Georg Philipp Telemann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Heinrich_Graun" title="Carl Heinrich Graun">Carl Heinrich Graun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Vinci" title="Leonardo Vinci">Leonardo Vinci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Gallus" title="Jacobus Gallus">Jacobus Gallus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Antonio_Urio" title="Francesco Antonio Urio">Francesco Antonio Urio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reinhard_Keiser" title="Reinhard Keiser">Reinhard Keiser</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Gasparini" title="Francesco Gasparini">Francesco Gasparini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Bononcini" title="Giovanni Bononcini">Giovanni Bononcini</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Boyce_(composer)" title="William Boyce (composer)">William Boyce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Lawes" title="Henry Lawes">Henry Lawes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Wise" title="Michael Wise">Michael Wise</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agostino_Steffani" title="Agostino Steffani">Agostino Steffani</a>, Franz Johann Habermann, and numerous others.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In an essay published in 1985, John H. Roberts demonstrated that Handel's borrowings were unusually frequent even for his own era, enough to have been criticised by contemporaries (notably <a href="/wiki/Johann_Mattheson" title="Johann Mattheson">Johann Mattheson</a>); Roberts suggested several reasons for Handel's practice, including Handel's attempts to make certain works sound more up-to-date and more radically, his "basic lack of facility in inventing original ideas" – though Roberts took care to argue that this does not "diminish Handel's stature", which should be "judged not by his methods, still less by his motives in employing them, but solely by the effects he achieves."<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homages">Homages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Homages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster-Handel-Commemoration-1784.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Westminster-Handel-Commemoration-1784.png/170px-Westminster-Handel-Commemoration-1784.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Westminster-Handel-Commemoration-1784.png/255px-Westminster-Handel-Commemoration-1784.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Westminster-Handel-Commemoration-1784.png/340px-Westminster-Handel-Commemoration-1784.png 2x" data-file-width="524" data-file-height="657" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster-Handel-Commemoration-1784.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The chorus, orchestra and organ in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, London during the <a href="/wiki/Handel_Commemoration" title="Handel Commemoration">Handel Commemoration</a> in 1784</div></div></div>
<p>After Handel's death, many composers wrote works based on or inspired by his music. The first movement from <a href="/wiki/Louis_Spohr" title="Louis Spohr">Louis Spohr</a>'s <i>Symphony No. 6</i>, Op. 116, "The Age of Bach and Handel", resembles two melodies from Handel's <i>Messiah</i>. In 1797 <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a> published the <i>12 Variations in G major on ‘See the conqu’ring hero comes’ from Judas Maccabaeus by Handel</i>, for cello and piano. In 1822 Beethoven composed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Consecration_of_the_House" class="mw-redirect" title="The Consecration of the House">The Consecration of the House</a></i> overture, which also bears the influence of Handel. Guitar virtuoso <a href="/wiki/Mauro_Giuliani" title="Mauro Giuliani">Mauro Giuliani</a> composed his <i>Variations on a Theme by Handel, Op. 107</i> for guitar, based on Handel's Suite No. 5 in E major, HWV 430, for harpsichord.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Handel-Westminster.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Handel-Westminster.png/170px-Handel-Westminster.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Handel-Westminster.png/255px-Handel-Westminster.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Handel-Westminster.png/340px-Handel-Westminster.png 2x" data-file-width="403" data-file-height="627" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Handel-Westminster.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Handel's monument in Westminster Abbey with the plaque recording his <a href="/wiki/Handel_Commemoration" title="Handel Commemoration">commemoration</a> in 1784</div></div></div>
<p>In 1861, using a theme from the second of Handel's harpsichord suites, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Johannes Brahms</a> wrote the <i><a href="/wiki/Variations_and_Fugue_on_a_Theme_by_Handel" title="Variations and Fugue on a Theme by Handel">Variations and Fugue on a Theme by Handel</a></i>, Op. 24, one of his most successful works (praised by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>).  Several works by the French composer <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix-Alexandre_Guilmant" class="mw-redirect" title="Félix-Alexandre Guilmant">Félix-Alexandre Guilmant</a> use Handel's themes, for example his <i>March on a Theme by Handel</i> uses a theme from <i>Messiah</i>. French composer and flautist <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Gaubert" title="Philippe Gaubert">Philippe Gaubert</a> wrote his <i>Petite marche</i> for flute and piano based on the fourth movement of Handel's Trio Sonata, Op. 5, No. 2, HWV 397. <a href="/wiki/Argentine_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Argentine people">Argentine</a> composer Luis Gianneo composed his <i>Variations on a Theme by Handel</i> for piano. In 1911, Australian-born composer and pianist <a href="/wiki/Percy_Grainger" title="Percy Grainger">Percy Grainger</a> based one of his most famous works on the final movement of Handel's Suite No. 5 in E major (just like Giuliani). He first wrote some variations on the theme, which he titled <i>Variations on Handel's 'The Harmonious Blacksmith' </i>. Then he used the first sixteen bars of his set of variations to create <i>Handel in the Strand</i>, one of his most beloved pieces, of which he made several versions (for example, the piano solo version from 1930). <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schoenberg" title="Arnold Schoenberg">Arnold Schoenberg</a>'s Concerto for String Quartet and Orchestra in B-flat major (1933) was composed after Handel's Concerto Grosso, Op. 6/7.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Veneration">Veneration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Veneration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Handel is honoured with a <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast day</a> on 28 July in the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Episcopal_Church)" title="Calendar of saints (Episcopal Church)">liturgical calendar of the Episcopal Church</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Purcell" title="Henry Purcell">Henry Purcell</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Lutheran)" title="Calendar of saints (Lutheran)">Lutheran Calendar of Saints</a> Handel and Bach share that date with <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Sch%C3%BCtz" title="Heinrich Schütz">Heinrich Schütz</a>, and Handel and Bach are commemorated in the calendar of saints prepared by the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Saint_Luke" title="Order of Saint Luke">Order of Saint Luke</a> for the use of the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fictional_depictions">Fictional depictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Fictional depictions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main page: <a href="/wiki/Category:George_Frideric_Handel_in_fiction" title="Category:George Frideric Handel in fiction">Category:George Frideric Handel in fiction</a></div>
<p>In 1942, Handel was the subject of the British <a href="/wiki/Biopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Biopic">biopic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Mr._Handel" title="The Great Mr. Handel">The Great Mr. Handel</a></i> directed by <a href="/wiki/Norman_Walker_(director)" title="Norman Walker (director)">Norman Walker</a> and starring <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid_Lawson_(actor)" title="Wilfrid Lawson (actor)">Wilfrid Lawson</a>. It was made at <a href="/wiki/Denham_Studios" class="mw-redirect" title="Denham Studios">Denham Studios</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Rank_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Rank Organisation">Rank Organisation</a>, and shot in <a href="/wiki/Technicolor" title="Technicolor">Technicolor</a>. He is also the central character in the television films <i><a href="/wiki/God_Rot_Tunbridge_Wells!" title="God Rot Tunbridge Wells!">God Rot Tunbridge Wells!</a></i> (1985) and <i><a href="/wiki/Handel%27s_Last_Chance" title="Handel&#39;s Last Chance">Handel's Last Chance</a></i> (1996) and the stage play <i><a href="/wiki/All_the_Angels" title="All the Angels">All the Angels</a></i> (2015). Handel was portrayed as the antagonist in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Farinelli_(film)" title="Farinelli (film)">Farinelli</a></i> (1994).
</p>

<h2><span id="Notes.2C_references_and_sources"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes,_references_and_sources">Notes, references and sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Notes, references and sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.collinsdictionary.com/dictionary/english/handel">"Handel"</a> entry in <i><a href="/wiki/Collins_English_Dictionary" title="Collins English Dictionary">Collins English Dictionary</a></i> gives the common variant "George Frederick" (used in his will and on his funeral monument) alongside the pronunciation of his last name. The spelling "Frideric" is used on his 1727 application for British citizenship.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to baptismal records in Halle's parish church, the Lutheran <a href="/wiki/Marktkirche_Unser_Lieben_Frauen" title="Marktkirche Unser Lieben Frauen">Marktkirche Unser Lieben Frauen</a>. The records of that church also show that the family name was spelled on various occasions at least four other ways: Hendel, Händeler, Hendeler and Hendtler, but most commonly Händel. In Italy he spelled it Hendel, as it is pronounced in German. From the time he arrived in England, however, he consistently signed his name as George Frideric Handel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHogwood19841_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHogwood19841-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Handel's tomb in Westminster has the birth date of 23 February 1684, using the Annunciation style of the beginning of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchoelcher18572_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchoelcher18572-5">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Among the court musicians of Halle were <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Scheidt" title="Samuel Scheidt">Samuel Scheidt</a> (who also was organist at the Moritzkirche), <a href="/wiki/William_Brade" title="William Brade">William Brade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Praetorius" title="Michael Praetorius">Michael Praetorius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrows19941_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrows19941-15">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Halle also was noted for the quality of its organ-builders. In 1712, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Bach</a> was intrigued by the organ at Marktkirche, and applied for the position that Zachow, Handel's teacher, vacated. He decided on <a href="/wiki/Weimar" title="Weimar">Weimar</a>, however.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang196620_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELang196620-17">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This barber, Andreas Berger, happened to be the son-in-law of English <i>émigré</i> William Brade, court musician to Augustus in Weissenfels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamsHofestädt2005144_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamsHofestädt2005144-13">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Both Landon and Hogwood point out and to the extent possible correct the more obvious misstatements of facts and dates and inconsistencies of Mainwaring. <i>See</i> <a href="#CITEREFLandon1984">Landon 1984</a>, pp.&#160;9–19; <a href="#CITEREFHogwood1984">Hogwood 1984</a>, pp.&#160;11–17.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Victor_Sch%C5%93lcher" title="Victor Schœlcher">Schoelcher</a> suggests that Handel's "doctor" father observed Handel making musical sounds even before he could talk and this in the eyes of the son of a coppersmith "discovered instincts of so low an order …"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchoelcher18573_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchoelcher18573-31">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The year and purpose of the visit and why the meeting occurred is variously given. Schoelcher and Bone have it that Handel was seven and they were visiting a son by Georg's first marriage, who was in service to the Duke.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Chrysander" title="Friedrich Chrysander">Friedrich Chrysander</a> states that they were visiting the younger Handel's nephew, Carl (ten years his senior) who was the Duke's valet.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Lang writes that Handel was nine and Handel's father, holding a court position, must have frequently travelled to Weissenfels, where the Duke had established a residence after <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> had annexed the city of Halle. Young Handel was taken along because he could be cared for by relatives of his late wife.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang196611_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELang196611-36">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">His cantatas, often highly dramatic, are distinguished by very imaginative choral writing, colorful orchestration, and skilful handling of the concerted element."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang196611–12_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELang196611–12-44">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Handel not only applied Kerll's techniques and phrases in later compositions, he imported an entire movement composed by Kerll into <a href="/wiki/Israel_in_Egypt" title="Israel in Egypt">Israel in Egypt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang196614_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELang196614-50">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Both Landon and Hogwood point out and to the extent possible correct the more obvious misstatements of facts and dates and inconsistencies of Mainwaring. <i>See</i> <a href="#CITEREFLandon1984">Landon 1984</a>, pp.&#160;9–19; <a href="#CITEREFHogwood1984">Hogwood 1984</a>, pp.&#160;11–17.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There was no "king" in Berlin until 18 January 1701 when Frederick III, the Elector of Brandenburg, became <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I_of_Prussia" title="Frederick I of Prussia">Frederick I</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/King_in_Prussia" title="King in Prussia">King in Prussia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandon198430_n.5_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandon198430_n.5-60">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Among the careful authorities who accepted the trip taking place in 1698 were Handel's friend <a href="/wiki/Johann_Mattheson" title="Johann Mattheson">Johann Mattheson</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang1966166_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELang1966166-64">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and Burney.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchoelcher18576_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchoelcher18576-48">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Records of the Marktkirche show that he took communion there in April of the years 1701–3.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Handel was required by the terms of his appointment, among other things, "to play the organ fittingly at Divine Service, and for this purpose to pre-intone the prescribed Psalms and Spiritual Songs, and to have due care to whatever might be needful to the support of beautiful harmony …"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeutsch19559_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeutsch19559-79">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first mention of Handel from the time he took his last communion at the Marktkirche on 23 April<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeutsch19559_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeutsch19559-79">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> is in Mattheson's annotated translation of Mainwaring (published in 1761) where he writes that he met Handel in the Organ loft of the Church of St. Mary Magdalena in Hamburg.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeutsch195510_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeutsch195510-87">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In his earlier <i>Grunlage</i> (published in 1740), he fixes the date as 9 July.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMattheson174029,_191_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMattheson174029,_191-88">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mainwaring gives the cryptic explanation that since he had to earn a living from his profession, he had to find a place less distant than Berlin. Given that Hamburg's opera house was second only to Berlin's in repute, "it was resolved to send him thither on his own bottom, and chiefly with a view to improvement."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMainwaring176027–28_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMainwaring176027–28-90">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The passage suggests that Handel had already determined on secular dramatic music as a career, but who it was "to send him thither" is not explained.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-119">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 2000, the upper stories of 25 <a href="/wiki/Brook_Street" title="Brook Street">Brook Street</a> were leased to the Handel House Trust, and after extensive restoration, the Handel House Museum opened to the public with an events programme of baroque music.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.parliament.uk/business/publications/parliamentary-archives/archives-highlights/handel-and-naturalisation/">"British Citizen by Act of Parliament: George Frideric Handel"</a>. <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scores_and_recordings">Scores and recordings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Frideric_Handel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Scores and recordings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="https://imslp.org/wiki/Category:Handel,_George_Frideric" class="extiw" title="scores:Category:Handel, George Frideric">Free scores by George Frideric Handel</a> at the <a href="/wiki/International_Music_Score_Library_Project" title="International Music Score Library Project">International Music Score Library Project</a> (IMSLP): includes Complete Works Edition (<i><span lang="de" style="font-style:normal;" title="German language text">Ausgabe der Deutschen Händelgesellschaft</span></i>)</li>

<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mutopiaproject.org/cgibin/make-table.cgi?Composer=HandelGF&amp;preview=1">The Mutopia Project</a> provides free downloading of sheet music and MIDI files for some of Handel's works.</li>
<li>Free typeset <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://cantorion.org/musicsearch/composer/handel/">sheet music</a> of Handel's works from <i>Cantorion.org</i></li>
